<div id="review_1103226572" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1103226572" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36819318-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485843897p2/36819318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1103226572?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/36819318-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1103226572" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9502260767330102989">Be warned. This book is hard-core, gateway reading-crack. Science fiction loving readers will be incurably hooked, staying up late, letting their social life decay and cravenly devouring all the Iain M. Banks they can get their hands on in a desperate, sleep-deprived book-orgy. Well, that’s what happened to me anyway. <br/><br/>As is obvious, I loved this book. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> is everything I want in science fiction, and it is where my passionate love of Iain Banks’ work began. This is big concept, spraw</span>
  <span id="freeText9502260767330102989" style="display:none">Be warned. This book is hard-core, gateway reading-crack. Science fiction loving readers will be incurably hooked, staying up late, letting their social life decay and cravenly devouring all the Iain M. Banks they can get their hands on in a desperate, sleep-deprived book-orgy. Well, that’s what happened to me anyway. <br /><br />As is obvious, I loved this book. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> is everything I want in science fiction, and it is where my passionate love of Iain Banks’ work began. This is big concept, sprawling stuff, and Banks has both the imaginative chops and the writing skills to make it a must read.<br /><br />The central character -Horza, a shape shifting assassin - is recruited by a warlike race of tri-legged religious-extremist reptiles – The Idirans – to help them in their brutal war against a vast, previously peaceful society known as The Culture, a post-scarcity near utopia run in tandem by humans and machines. The Culture looks like a pretty cool place to live – no poverty, disease or violence, liberal attitudes to sex (including bodily modifications to make the act more pleasurable) and inbuilt glands in every citizen that release various drugs on demand. The Idirans are not so fun, but they’re pretty motivated in their religious fanatacism, and they are pushing the Culture hard in the war.<br /><br />At the beginning of the narrative a new variant of the AIs that run the Culture, known as Minds, escapes an Idiran attack and hides itself on a graveyard world home to a being so terrifying that neither side dares to approach it. As the story unfolds Horza pursues this Mind across space, competing with Culture efforts to retrieve it and travelling through a scintillating and baroque series of places and worlds that sent my imagination spinning.<br /><br />This is first-rate stuff. Banks has something very rare in SF – both a sense of humour and the skill to bring that humor to the page. I’ve laughed out loud reading Banks, and that puts him in a very, very small group of SF writers. Don’t misinterpret this however – Banks is no Pratchett. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> is serious SF, and there are numerous moments of horror and sadness in Banks’ universe that make his occasional bursts of humor stand out all the more. <br /><br />Of course, this is just the beginning of a landmark SF series. There are nine more Culture novels, and each of them is well worth your time. Other than telling you to buy a copy of <i>Consider Phlebas</i> immediately my only other advice regarding Banks' work is to try to pace yourself. I binge-read the entire Culture series, and now that Banks has passed away I regret that I have no further of his works to look forward to. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9502260767330102989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23763462" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23763462" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/273793-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1187069328p2/273793.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23763462?book_show_action=true">Jun 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/273793-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/273793-mark?shelf=sf">sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23763462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14168301283828364694">I usually like Iain Banks' sf novels, but this is simply a bad book.<br/><br/>It is partly an action novel, with the plot roughly "go to planet X and retrieve an advanced piece of technology." There are a few very exciting action sequences. The major problem is that after setting up the plot by page 4, we have a diversion of about 300 pages before returning to the plot. ??? The vast majority of the in-between chapters feel like a bunch of half-realized short story ideas jammed together, including one chap</span>
  <span id="freeText14168301283828364694" style="display:none">I usually like Iain Banks' sf novels, but this is simply a bad book.<br /><br />It is partly an action novel, with the plot roughly "go to planet X and retrieve an advanced piece of technology." There are a few very exciting action sequences. The major problem is that after setting up the plot by page 4, we have a diversion of about 300 pages before returning to the plot. ??? The vast majority of the in-between chapters feel like a bunch of half-realized short story ideas jammed together, including one chapter whose sole purpose is to be be disgusting. Ugh.<br /><br />I want to believe Banks was also trying to accomplish something beyond an action novel. Maybe developing a deep main character? Except the main character is "irrational hatred of computers" + "feels tender about women he feels tender about". That's it. Big whoop. Or here's another character: on the outside it seems like a single-minded alien soldier, but on the inside we find it is really, well, a single-minded alien soldier. I think we can scratch "deep characterization" off the list of possible positives.<br /><br />Banks does a few good things in terms of creating a universe, but the fundamental disjointedness of the book undermines the attempt to describe a cohesive world.<br /><br />And a final pet peeve -- bringing a character back from the dead is 95% likely to be a bad move. Doing it needlessly is 99.9% likely to be a bad move. Just...don't.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14168301283828364694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_61318700" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61318700" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2465351-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324174664p2/2465351.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/61318700?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/2465351-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781445007649" href="/book/show/11333657-consider-phlebas">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=fits-like-a-glove">fits-like-a-glove</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=love-strong-women">love-strong-women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2465351-ian?shelf=the-culture">the-culture</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61318700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9419684967456030894">I read all of Iain Banks' Culture books in order in which they were written, beginning with <i>Consider Phlebas</i> and ending with the latest, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7937744.Surface_Detail' title='Surface Detail by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Surface Detail</a>, from 2008 through 2010. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> being the first Culture book Banks wrote, it was the first I read back in the Spring of 2008. I liked it. One might even say I liked it a lot. But I didn't love it. Not yet.<br/><br/>I just re-read <i>Consider Phlebas</i> and I can tell you it’s a whole different book when you have the entire collection under your belt. T</span>
  <span id="freeText9419684967456030894" style="display:none">I read all of Iain Banks' Culture books in order in which they were written, beginning with <i>Consider Phlebas</i> and ending with the latest, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7937744.Surface_Detail" title="Surface Detail by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Surface Detail</a>, from 2008 through 2010. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> being the first Culture book Banks wrote, it was the first I read back in the Spring of 2008. I liked it. One might even say I liked it a lot. But I didn't love it. Not yet.<br /><br />I just re-read <i>Consider Phlebas</i> and I can tell you it’s a whole different book when you have the entire collection under your belt. That’s when the brilliance of <i>Consider Phlebas</i> begins to shine. That’s when you recognize the foreshadowing and prescience, and, even more impressive in my eyes, the acknowledgement of, and responses to, objections that people would raise to the whole idea of the Culture, or at least the Culture as conceived and fleshed out by Iain Banks, objections that people wouldn’t raise until later books like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12016.Look_to_Windward" title="Look to Windward by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Look to Windward</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/886066.Matter" title="Matter by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Matter</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7937744.Surface_Detail" title="Surface Detail by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Surface Detail</a> come along. The protagonist in <i>Consider Phlebas</i> raises all those objections himself, and argues with himself about their merit, about the Culture’s merit.<br /><br />There’s another, more potent aspect of <i>Consider Phlebas</i> that makes it more powerful when read the second time, and it has to do with knowing how the book ends, so here I’m forced to use the embedded spoiler function. Click at your own risk:<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[If you’ve clicked, I’m going to assume that you’ve read the book. As you know, the Changer dies. He dies just short of his goal, fighting for a purpose that doesn’t waiver even as he questions his motivations, fighting alongside his nemesis even as he questions whether she is truly an enemy. We also learn in the appendix that his race is wiped out in the course of the Idiran War. Knowing how this tragedy plays out made my reading of the Changer’s final months more ... I’m not sure of the right words ... more touching? compelling? moving? It was more <i>something</i> and I’m still figuring out what. Perhaps the word I want is <i>intimate</i>. I shared Horza’s final months, days, and hours, knowing what he didn’t, knowing what he feared, that both his love—the female Changer at the base—and himself were going to die at the hand of the very people for whom he was fighting. I shared his thoughts and feelings, knowing what he couldn’t. It reminded me of when my cat, Sebastian, died of a blood clot about two years ago. We had had him since he was a kitten and he was only nine years old at his death—a young death for a cat but he was with us long enough to be part of the family, for sure. I held him in my arms as the veterinarian injected the solution that would put him to sleep and stop his heart, ending his life. It was ... difficult, to say the least. It was also one of the most intimate experiences of my life, certainly the most intimate experience I’ve had with an animal, sharing his last moments, knowing he was dying and trying to make him feel loved right to the moment his eyes closed and his body went limp. It was all the harder since Sebastian didn’t know or understand what was happening. So Yes, I think intimate is the word I was looking for, at least as intimate as one can be with a fictional character. I hate that I even have to make this qualification because it should be obvious, but I’m <u>not</u> trying to say that reading <i>Consider Phlebas</i> was as emotional as my cat’s death; I’m merely drawing a parallel. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />If you haven’t clicked then I assume you haven’t read <i>Consider Phlebas</i>. I don’t know how to suggest that you read it. If you’ve read other Culture books, then definitely read <i>Consider Phlebas</i> at your earliest opportunity. If you haven’t read other Culture books, then I still say read <i>Consider Phlebas</i> first. Even though you may not get the full meaning it has to offer, it sets the stage. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> takes place during the Idiran War, the consequences of which would reverberate through the Culture for centuries to come. Then maybe read it again ;)<br />------------------------------------------------<br />P.S. This audiobook is once again read by Peter Kenney, who is the reader on many of Iain Banks' audiobooks. Mr. Kenney's pacing and voicing are excellent, as ususal.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9419684967456030894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_203455221" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203455221" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/203455221?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203455221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13116290114303919104"><i>“You’re ruled by your machines. You’re an evolutionary dead end. The trouble is that to take your mind off it you try to drag everybody else down there with you.”</i><br/><br/>Back to the beginning. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> is the first book of the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/49118-culture' rel='nofollow noopener'>Culture series</a> (ten volumes in total, I believe), one of the most beloved sci-fi book series ever, written by the late great <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5807106.Iain_M__Banks' title='Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'> Iain M. Banks</a>, feel free to confuse him with the equally late and great <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7628.Iain_Banks' title='Iain Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'> Iain Banks</a>, who is indeed the same writer but is described as a “literary </span>
  <span id="freeText13116290114303919104" style="display:none"><i>“You’re ruled by your machines. You’re an evolutionary dead end. The trouble is that to take your mind off it you try to drag everybody else down there with you.”</i><br><br>Back to the beginning. <i>Consider Phlebas</i> is the first book of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/49118-culture" rel="nofollow noopener">Culture series</a> (ten volumes in total, I believe), one of the most beloved sci-fi book series ever, written by the late great <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5807106.Iain_M__Banks" title="Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener"> Iain M. Banks</a>, feel free to confuse him with the equally late and great <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7628.Iain_Banks" title="Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener"> Iain Banks</a>, who is indeed the same writer but is described as a “literary novelist” and wrote mainstream non-sci-fi books. It is not surprising then that this series has a more literary, at times experimental, prose style than most sci-fi books, and is treasured by sci-fi connoisseurs.<br><br>In a nutshell, the Culture series is an epic space opera featuring a post-scarcity galaxy spanning group-civilization called “The Culture”, ostensibly owned by humans but actually run by super AI entities called The Minds. The humans live hedonistic carefree lives, everything they can possibly want is catered for by the AIs, the Minds, the drones (smaller AIs) and other high-tech devices. Banks avoids using the words computers and robots as the Minds and the drones are hyper-advanced sentient machines with personalities, they are living non-biological entities. The Culture series are all standalone books set in this universe, each volume tells a story concerning the humans and the machines of this powerful empire.<br><br><i>Consider Phlebas</i> is the story of protagonist Bora Horza Gobuchul, a Changer, a transhuman with an ability to gradually morph into other humans. “Horza” hates the Culture with a passion and has sided with the Idirans, an alien race at war with the Culture, a war of ideology. The Idirans think the Culture is too influential in the galaxy and too uppity with their mission to “improve the lives of those in less-advanced societies”, in direct opposition to the Idirans’ expansionist policy of conquering such societies through military might.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506523723i/24025172._SY540_.jpg" width="350" height="467" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>An Idiran<br><br>The main story arc concerns a Mind which crash-landed on a remote planet, the Culture and the Idirans are racing to retrieve/capture this Mind as it possesses a wealth of information that can be used against the Culture. Horza is employed by the Idirans for this mission and the narrative depicts his long, complicated and very dangerous mission involving an encounter with a race of cannibals, participating in a bizarre high-tech computer game, taking over a mercenary ship by taking over the captain’s identity, and the eventual confrontation with the Culture and his less than trustworthy employer, the Idirans. <br><br><i>Consider Phlebas</i> is a very entertaining, wild, and sometimes thrilling read. It is, however, a sprawling adventure with a rather episodic feel to most of the chapters. I do find some of the chapters to be a little longer than they need to be; I wish it was a tighter narrative. Having said that, I do not recall any actual dull moments, just that some scenes are less compelling than others. Characterization is definitely one of Bank’s strengths. This book features a complex, interesting, badass and, at times, hilarious characters. Horza is something of an anti-hero who does some reprehensible things throughout the narrative, but he also has his moments of heroism. Culture agent Perosteck Balveda is a very tenacious character with a strong sense of morality, but best of all is the sarcastic longsuffering drone Unaha-Closp who outshines all the biological characters for me.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1506523651i/24025165._SX540_.jpg" width="350" height="279" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Unaha-Closp <a href="https://verox11.deviantart.com/art/Unaha-Closp-Culture-Drone-135187558" rel="nofollow noopener">by verox11</a> <br><br>Is <i>Consider Phlebas</i> the best Culture book, or the best one to start with? I would say no to both. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18630.The_Player_of_Games__Culture___2_" title="The Player of Games (Culture, #2) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener"> The Player of Games</a> is my favorite Culture book (so far, I have a few more to read), it is shorter, tighter, quite riveting and is generally wonderful; better still, you do not need to have read any other Culture novels to follow it. However, in and of itself, if what I wrote so longwindedly about this book so far seems enticing to you then yes, I can confirm it is a good and worthwhile read. The series itself I can certainly recommend as something unique that you should not do without. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1505098075i/23882260.jpg" alt="ray guns line" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Notes:</b><br>• If you want to read some background information about the Culture series, read Banks’ own guide: <a href="http://www.vavatch.co.uk/books/banks/cultnote.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">A Few Notes on the Culture</a>. You can, of course, just dive into any one of the books.<br><br>• I was watching <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/36065956.Star_Trek_Discovery__1" title="Star Trek Discovery #1 by Mike Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener"> Star Trek: Discovery</a> last night and it occurred to me that the Culture is similar to Trek’s Federation in some ways. Both include advanced utopian human civilizations, and respected by alien races. Though The Culture appears to be primarily owned by humans and the most powerful civilization in the known galaxy.<br><br><b>Quotes:</b><br><br><i>“What do you want? A robot?” Its voice sneered. “I don’t have an Off button on my reasoning functions; I can’t choose not to have free will. I could quite easily swear to obey all orders regardless of the consequences; I could vow to sacrifice my life for you if you asked me to; but I’d be lying, so that I could live.”<br><br>“But the Culture, that seemingly disunited, anarchic, hedonistic, decadent mélange of more or less human species, forever hiving off or absorbing different groups of people, had fought for almost four years without showing any sign of giving up or even coming to a compromise.”</i><br><br>(On the game, “Damage”) <i>“It’s been called the most decadent game in history. About all you can say in the game’s defense is that it, rather than reality, occupies the warped minds of some of the galaxy’s more twisted people; gods know what they would get up to if it wasn’t there.”</i><br><br><i>“They had their communist Utopia. They were soft and pampered and indulged, and the Contact section’s evangelical materialism provided their conscience-salving good works.”<br><br>“Individuality, the thing which most humans held more precious than anything else about themselves, was somehow cheapened by the ease with which a Changer could ignore it as a limitation and use it as a disguise.”</i><br><br>(On the Minds) <i>“They were so intelligent that no human was capable of understanding just how smart they were (and the machines themselves were incapable of describing it to such a limited form of life).” </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13116290114303919104" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_83138725" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/83138725" />
    <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar"><img alt="Dirk Grobbelaar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545117991p2/3102740.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/83138725?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dirk Grobbelaar" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar">Dirk Grobbelaar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316005388" href="/book/show/535073.Consider_Phlebas">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer83138725" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11911166790080415733">Interestingly, while this is the first <i>Culture</i> novel, it is told from the perspective of a somewhat antagonistic protagonist. Bora Horza Gobuchul opposes the Culture. Vehemently.<br/> <br/>While the novel, arguably, isn’t quite as sophisticated as later entries in the series, it sucked me right in. It certainly is a lot of fun, and feels more action based than most of the books that came later. It’s no surprise that many reviewers prefer the later entries in the series to this one (they are somewhat more </span>
  <span id="freeText11911166790080415733" style="display:none">Interestingly, while this is the first <i>Culture</i> novel, it is told from the perspective of a somewhat antagonistic protagonist. Bora Horza Gobuchul opposes the Culture. Vehemently.<br /> <br />While the novel, arguably, isn’t quite as sophisticated as later entries in the series, it sucked me right in. It certainly is a lot of fun, and feels more action based than most of the books that came later. It’s no surprise that many reviewers prefer the later entries in the series to this one (they are somewhat more literary focused with lots of intellectual aspects to chew on), but I have a lot of love for <i>Consider Phlebas</i>. It’s a quicker read, and much less demanding than, say, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12007.Use_of_Weapons" title="Use of Weapons by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Use of Weapons</a>, while the latter packs a bigger psychological punch. Again, I enjoyed both, but for different reasons.<br /><br />There’s quite a bit happening here, with characters hopping from one exotic location to the next in a series of adventures leading to an inevitable Banksian conclusion. If you’re a serious Science Fiction fan you are probably going to read the <i>Culture</i> series at some point (if you haven’t done so already) and this is still the logical place to start. While the novels are standalone, there is an overarching theme of moral conflict and political commentary, and it starts right here. Don’t let the more conventional nature and high adventure of this one fool you into thinking there isn’t something more beneath the surface, this is still Banks we’re talking about. There are other reviewers here who have pointed out the academic considerations much more appropriately than I can, so I’ll leave it at that.<br /><br />In closing. I enjoyed it. A lot. It’s a fine example of Space Opera and it opened up the whole Culture Universe for me. <br /><br /><u>Update</u><br /><br />As of 2018 it seems that there is a TV series being developed for this. Wonder how that’s going to pan out? </span>
  <a data-text-id="11911166790080415733" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_145698143" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145698143" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4715802-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430904434p2/4715802.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/145698143?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/4715802-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">as a starting point in the Culture series</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer145698143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10510728959402663341">The opening scene to Iain M. Banks' opening novel in the Culture universe is one of my favourites in sci-fi. How would an ultra-sophisticated artificial intelligence escape certain death at the hands of an enemy? What moves would it make? What sacrifices?<br/><br/>The very next scene, in which we meet our protagonist Horza, is a huge win. Remember when we met Aragorn in Lord of the Rings? There was practically a drum roll. Yeah, well there's none of that here. Horza is being slowly put to death by drownin</span>
  <span id="freeText10510728959402663341" style="display:none">The opening scene to Iain M. Banks' opening novel in the Culture universe is one of my favourites in sci-fi. How would an ultra-sophisticated artificial intelligence escape certain death at the hands of an enemy? What moves would it make? What sacrifices?<br /><br />The very next scene, in which we meet our protagonist Horza, is a huge win. Remember when we met Aragorn in Lord of the Rings? There was practically a drum roll. Yeah, well there's none of that here. Horza is being slowly put to death by drowning in the enemy's sewer system. Blagh. I've read a couple of Goodreviews that write this scene off as extraneous or at best irrelevant. To the contrary, I would argue that it gives you a nice idea about how easy the rest of Horza's travels are going to be and sets the dark(ish) yet playful mood. <br /><br />Horza is himself rather interesting. He has unique physical capabilities, is introspective, and extremely resourceful. He laments many things. Consider Phlebas basically covers his Indiana Jones-type adventures. <br /><br />Banks has made unusual choices in his viewpoint characters in all three of his novels that I've read: an enemy of the Culture (Consider Phlebas), a bored gamer who temporarily leaves the Culture <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145210672" rel="nofollow noopener">(Player of Games)</a> and a non-Culture agent who does their dirty work <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/136992238" rel="nofollow noopener">(Use of Weapons)</a>. We're always on the edge of the Culture itself, and only get hints of it as background, foreground and texture. I find that technique effective for a couple of reasons. First, as others have remarked, it wouldn't be much fun to read about the interior utopia of the Culture itself: "Woke up on silk sheets, didn't work, ate for free, worked a bit cause I liked it, played a game, glanded some wicked-bad drugs, went to bed." Also, seeing glimpses of something is always more interesting than being exposed to its raw nature. The influence of the Culture is constant, if distant. Sounds like most governments.<br /><br />Banks' ability to describe scale is also impressive. In my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137003420" rel="nofollow noopener">Ringworld</a>, I argue that Larry Niven failed to take advantage of his own wonderful construct of the Ring. I found his exploration of this incredibly impressive artifact to be a snooze ride. Banks picks up where Niven leaves off. In particular, Horza's flight through the General Systems Vehicle, which are typically between 25 and 200 km in each dimension, was some of the best action I've read in a while.<br /><br />I take this opportunity to highlight Banks' deliciously subtle humour. Due to their complexity, the starships in the Culture universe are inhabited and controlled by a Mind(s). The inert and stale names given to the starships by humans, such as General Systems Vehicle and Rapid Offensive Unit, are contrasted wildly and wonderfully with the names that the Minds give themselves and therefore the ships. Some examples include No More Mr. Nice Guy; Profit Margin; Prosthetic Conscience; Nervous Energy; and Sweet and Full of Grace.<br /><br />I've made the opposite of the following comment in my review of Greg Bear's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/142587610" rel="nofollow noopener">Eon</a>, but I feel with this worth repeating. Although Phlebas was published in 1987, in no way does it feel outdated or Gorbachev-like. The politics feel fresh, sharp and modern. Consider Phlebas was my introduction to the Culture and what an introduction it was. Whether Banks' series is my favourite sci-fi is still up in the air as I go through the books. However, I can say that the Culture is one of the most well thought out, interesting and multi-faceted political systems/civilizations in modern speculative fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10510728959402663341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3412131930" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3412131930" />
    <a title="Melissa (Mel’s Bookshelf)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9185509-melissa-mel-s-bookshelf"><img alt="Melissa (Mel’s Bookshelf)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1540171256p2/9185509.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3412131930?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa (Mel’s Bookshelf)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa (Mel’s Bookshelf)" href="/user/show/9185509-melissa-mel-s-bookshelf">Melissa (Mel’s Bookshelf)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9185509-melissa-mel-s-bookshelf?shelf=read-along">read-along</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9185509-melissa-mel-s-bookshelf?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9185509-melissa-mel-s-bookshelf?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3412131930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5691475107052349593">I love a good sci-fi. Something to get me out of this world and into the universe for a while. And although this was a solidly good read. I guess I was expecting more from what has been dubbed by many as the best sci-fi series of all time.<br/><br/>I read this one as part of a youtube culture series read-along I am doing with Moid and his followers on Media Death Cult (awesome channel, check it out if you are a sci-fi fan!) and I did a bit of research leading up to this one. Many people say it is the wors</span>
  <span id="freeText5691475107052349593" style="display:none">I love a good sci-fi. Something to get me out of this world and into the universe for a while. And although this was a solidly good read. I guess I was expecting more from what has been dubbed by many as the best sci-fi series of all time.<br /><br />I read this one as part of a youtube culture series read-along I am doing with Moid and his followers on Media Death Cult (awesome channel, check it out if you are a sci-fi fan!) and I did a bit of research leading up to this one. Many people say it is the worst of The Culture series. That it is more of a space-opera rather than a hardcore sci-fi, and that it lacks the substance of the remaining books. Some people even go as far as to say you should not start this series by reading this one even though it was the first one published. The Culture series are mostly all stand-alone books so can theoretically be read in any order, but I am a stickler for reading things from the beginning.<br /><br />It just fell flat a little for me. There were some great things about it and some not-so-great. So I am going to change from my normal review formatting style and use some bullet points for a change. And I’m going to start with the things I didn’t enjoy:<br /><br /><b>The Bad</b><br />- I originally purchased the audio version. Although I didn’t mind the narrator too much, I found that I was distracted from the storyline VERY easily. Even the mundane tasks of washing the dishes (our dishwasher broke down OMG) and hanging out the clothes did not allow me to cling to every word unfortunately. So I sent it back in favour of the kindle version.<br />- There were some LONG SLOW parts. More than I would have liked to be honest, even the action scenes were not particularly exciting.<br />- I didn’t warm to the characters very well.<br />- How it ended. Well, let’s just say I won't be expecting a happy endings in the next books!<br /><br /><b>The Good</b><br />- The premise. WOW I can see that there is going to be so much more to this universe and all the different technologies and politics. I absolutely loved the culture/Idirians conflict and am excited to see where it goes and who the good-guys actually are!<br />- I loved the weird tangents it goes on! The section where Horza is captured by the strange cult was one of the highlights of the book for me.<br />- The writing was great. Easy to read, even through the slow parts.<br />- A good introduction and teaser for what is to come, I hope.<br /><br /><b>Would I recommend Consider Phlebas?</b><br />I don’t know yet. As a total stand alone it was a little flat, but as an intro to a universe in a series, it shows a lot of promise. I will be able to give a better rationale behind my recommendation after I have read a few more to get a wider perspective.<br /><br /><i>I purchased Consider Phlebas at my own expense.</i><br /><br />For more reviews check out my: <br /><a href="http://www.melsbookshelf.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> <br /><a href="http://www.instagram.com/mels_book_shelf" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> <br /><a href="http://www.twitter.com/melsbookshelf" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a>  <br /><a href="http://www.facebook.com/melsbookshelfAU" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> </span>

<div id="review_2739222465" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2739222465" />
    <a title="Gavin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8192509-gavin"><img alt="Gavin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338207884p2/8192509.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2739222465?book_show_action=true">Mar 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gavin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gavin" href="/user/show/8192509-gavin">Gavin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8192509-gavin?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2739222465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6139524140631224614">I enjoyed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory' title='The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wasp Factory</a> when I read it a few years back so had fairly high hopes for this sci-fi series of Banks as I loved the premise of the story. The bad news is the story failed to live up to my high expectations and ended up being a total disappointment that even became a bit of a chore to finish! <br/><br/>Consider Phlebas really should have been my sort of book as the ideas behind the world were fantastic, the choice of unique main character was good, and the story had a good mix of political i</span>
  <span id="freeText6139524140631224614" style="display:none">I enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory" title="The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wasp Factory</a> when I read it a few years back so had fairly high hopes for this sci-fi series of Banks as I loved the premise of the story. The bad news is the story failed to live up to my high expectations and ended up being a total disappointment that even became a bit of a chore to finish! <br /><br />Consider Phlebas really should have been my sort of book as the ideas behind the world were fantastic, the choice of unique main character was good, and the story had a good mix of political intrigue and action. I'm not quite sure how it all went so wrong as the start was really good. I was sucked in right away and intrigued by the world and story. It all went wrong as soon as the space pirates hit the story and never got back on track. <br /><br />The Good<br /><br />-The Worldbuilding. Banks world was a fascinating one. The story was set in the far future populated by multiple human societies and a few alien ones. The story kicked off as the alien Idiran race were taking the war to the Culture. To complicate things a few other human and alien factions were drawn into the conflict. The Idrian were a race that was both superior physically to humans as well as a lot longer lived. They were also fanatically devoted to their religion. I found them interesting enough but the real fun in the story came from the Culture. The Culture was a liberal socialist society that was governed by AI. What an awesome concept! I'm not at all surprised that it was a Scot that came up with such a fun potential society. <br /><br />-Unique Main Character. The main character had the potential to be a fantastic one. Horza was a human but no ordinary one as he was a shape changer that worked as a spy and infiltrator for the Idrians. His home world had been conquered by the Idrians a bunch of years before the story began and he was a willing collaborator with the Idrians as he saw them as better bet than AI. The Changers were human but a splinter society that had undergone a lot of genetic modification to make them more than human. I enjoyed how Banks did not shy from the fact that humans would use technology to advance or improve their bodies if it became a possibility. <br /><br />-The AI. We met a couple of sentient AI minds in the story and they were more interesting than the "living" characters! <br /><br />The Bad<br /><br />-Horza. Horza should have been an awesome main character and to be fair he did start out that way. I was caught up in his story and intrigued by him for the first 20% of the story. Unfortunately he never quite built on that early intrigue and the more the story developed the more and more I grew both bored and disinterested in him and his story. It did not help that he was not particularly likeable. <br /><br />-Lack of Emotional Investment. My lack of emotional investment in Horza or any of the secondary characters in the story was a big problem as it meant that when we were not learning titbits about the Culture I was bored as I could not care less about Horza's fate or that of any of the other characters in the story. <br /><br />-The Plot. I feel Banks made a poor call with the focus of the story. The would-building was great and the intrigue we got at the start of the story was a lot of fun. I feel that should have stayed the focus of the story but instead we got a bunch of mindless action with Horza blundering from one near death action scene to the next and it all got very samey and boring. This was a big part of the reason the book became a chore to finish. <br /><br />All in all I was really quite disappointed by what we got in this one. It had a cool sci-fi concept and a similar story and character set-up to what we got in the Wasp Factory but sadly Banks just never managed to make either work as well as they should have in this particular story and the end result was a story that got worse and worse as it progressed. It is a real pity as the Culture is the most fascinating future society I've encountered in ages and I'd have loved to learn a lot more about them than this story offered. <br /><br />The biggest condemnation of this whole book was the fact that the appendices at the end covering the Idrian War and the Culture ended up being a way more enjoyable read than the actual story! <br /><br />Rating: 2.5 stars. I'm rounding up to 3 stars here on Goodreads just for the ideas and the world-building. They were 5 star great but the plot and characters were 2 star drivel. <br /><br />Audio Note: Peter Kenny did a decent job with the audio but failed to bring the character and story to life in the same way he did with the Wasp Factory. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6139524140631224614" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18620404" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer214447871628675696">I'm not really sure what to say about <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_' title='Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Consider Phlebas</a>. It was, quite fittingly, the first Culture book I read, though it was my fourth Banks book (preceded by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory' title='The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wasp Factory</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12006.Dead_Air' title='Dead Air by Iain Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dead Air</a>, & <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/249606.The_Bridge_' title='The Bridge  by Iain Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Bridge</a> respectively). And now it is the third Banks book I've reread (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory' title='The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wasp Factory</a> twice, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12007.Use_of_Weapons__Culture___3_' title='Use of Weapons (Culture, #3) by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Use of Weapons</a> once).<br/><br/>I like it very much, so I feel a little sad that many friends I respect don't love it as much as I and a good deal of them just think it is mostly okay.<br/><br/>I love that Horza is an unlikable p</span>
  <span id="freeText214447871628675696" style="display:none">I'm not really sure what to say about <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_" title="Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a>. It was, quite fittingly, the first Culture book I read, though it was my fourth Banks book (preceded by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory" title="The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wasp Factory</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12006.Dead_Air" title="Dead Air by Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Dead Air</a>, &amp; <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/249606.The_Bridge_" title="The Bridge  by Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bridge</a> respectively). And now it is the third Banks book I've reread (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory" title="The Wasp Factory by Iain Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wasp Factory</a> twice, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12007.Use_of_Weapons__Culture___3_" title="Use of Weapons (Culture, #3) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Use of Weapons</a> once).<br /><br />I like it very much, so I feel a little sad that many friends I respect don't love it as much as I and a good deal of them just think it is mostly okay.<br /><br />I love that Horza is an unlikable protagonist, but I think that bothers some.<br /><br />I love that Banks delivers on the promise of his title and epigraph:<blockquote>IV. DEATH BY WATER<br /><br />Phlebas the Phoenician, a fortnight dead,<br />Forgot the cry of gulls, and the deep sea swell<br />And the profit and loss.<br />A current under sea<br />Picked his bones in whispers. As he rose and fell<br />He passed the stages of his age and youth<br />Entering the whirlpool.<br />Gentile or Jew<br />O you who turn the wheel and look to windward,<br />Consider Phlebas, who was once handsome and tall as you.</blockquote>But I am certain there are those who find the finish too bleak, and more than a little hopeless. <br /><br />Many are frustrated by Banks' propensity for long, detailed, action sequences and feel that these scenes disrupt the flow of the plot, whereas I love the cinematic quality of the action. <br /><br />A few don't like what they see as a choppy narrative -- more linked short stories than one cohesive novel. Again, I love the episodic nature, and I love the way we grow into our knowledge of the characters through these sporadic brushes with their lives. That works for me, and seems real -- sorta like the way I connect with all the friends who don't live with me on a daily basis. We brush by each other in episodes, and all we learn about each other is in our own little short stories of companionship.<br /><br />Some find <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_" title="Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a> too brutal. I think it is just brutal enough. <br /><br />Others can't find any character to relate to and pull for, or are only able to embrace one, but I find myself liking them all, even the most unsavoury, like Fwi-Song and Mr. First (although I wouldn't want to eat dinner with them ;)).<br /><br />But mostly I love what Banks is whispering in my ear while I read: "Hey, Brad. Heroes don't exist. Violence is our natural state, no matter who or what we are. Death comes to us all, somehow, someway, even seemingly immortal Minds. But that doesn't mean that life isn't beautiful. There is life in death, thus death matters. It makes life sweet, so don't forget to live it." For me, that's a message worth reading again and again. <br /><br />Yep, it's good to be reminded that my bag of skin is nothing but crude, decaying matter. There's humility in that, a humility that makes me look at the black ant crawling up my leg with brotherhood rather than disdain (and it really is, right now, this second). It reminds me to recognize our shared experience. And so I let him(it?) continue his(its?) walk and don't crush him(it?) between my thumb and forefinger. I simply let him(it?) get on his way. I hope I will always be able to do the same.<br /><br />(I wrote a review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/105193525" rel="nofollow noopener">Pippi Goes On Board</a> just after I finished reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_" title="Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a>. If you've read the latter you may like my review of the former. Check it out.)<br /><br />April 22, 2017 -- Been a while since I spent some time in Culture space, but I came back for a journey with the Clear Air Turbulence -- except this time I listened. I don't really want to add anything to my feelings about the text. I said it well enough above, but I do want to mention that it was a pleasure to listen to Peter Kenny's narration of the book. I'll be seeking his voice out in the future. </span>
  <a data-text-id="214447871628675696" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9133367259833472098">A frustrating book, perhaps just not my cup of tea. The writing did not click with me - I can't put my finger on it, but I kept being pulled out of the story because the writing felt bland and awkward. Considering the popularity of this series, I'm obviously in the minority in feeling this way. <br/><br/>I think that if I had enjoyed Banks's writing style, I wouldn't have objected to the slow pacing or the meandering storyline, but as it stands I thought most of the book was dull. The story seemed to be c</span>
  <span id="freeText9133367259833472098" style="display:none">A frustrating book, perhaps just not my cup of tea. The writing did not click with me - I can't put my finger on it, but I kept being pulled out of the story because the writing felt bland and awkward. Considering the popularity of this series, I'm obviously in the minority in feeling this way. <br /><br />I think that if I had enjoyed Banks's writing style, I wouldn't have objected to the slow pacing or the meandering storyline, but as it stands I thought most of the book was dull. The story seemed to be composed of pointless vignettes. Each time a new one started I would get excited: ooh, space pirates! Ooh, a giant orbital ring with floating megaships! Ooh, a life-or-death game of chance called Damage! But the narrative never lived up to my hopes, and I spent the whole book feeling disappointed. By the time the plot actually started to get interesting - the final sequence on Schar's World was admittedly suspenseful - I still didn't care about the characters.</span>

Of course, I've read only two books, and both seemed like slow, ponderous exercises.<br/><br/>This novel, for example, was recommend as "thinking man's sf adventure." Hey, that's appealing.   <span id="freeText4279300237534694747" style="display:none">People love their Iain Banks, and I respect that, though I've yet to read something of his that impresses me much. Of course, I've read only two books, and both seemed like slow, ponderous exercises.<br /><br />This novel, for example, was recommend as "thinking man's sf adventure." Hey, that's appealing. But this didn't strike me as that sort of book. Instead, it was slow-going, and lacked the giddy joy of invention and play that to my mind the best science fiction always has. I gritted my teeth and pushed my way through it and decided I was done with his sf. <br /><br />And then someone recommended <i>Complicity</i>, and that annoyed as well, so I am done with his thrillers, too. All that leaves are his mainstream "literary" novels. Cripes.</span>

<div id="review_180340422" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180340422" />
    <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes"><img alt="aPriL does feral sometimes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478243665p2/3409532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="aPriL does feral sometimes " href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes </a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer180340422" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17550115497897504690">'Consider Phlebas' by Iain M. Banks is the first novel in the Culture series. I loved it! This book is an amazing thrill ride of fast-paced entertainment as well as a fantastically imagined universe full of hard science technology.<br/><br/>The writing is so good it takes awhile before you realize the book's chapters consist of a series of tableaus that are sort of a tour of violence in this galaxy. It's exciting! Ok, that came out wrong. Moving on...<br/><br/>The science is the best part. All the cool body add-ons</span>
  <span id="freeText17550115497897504690" style="display:none">'Consider Phlebas' by Iain M. Banks is the first novel in the Culture series. I loved it! This book is an amazing thrill ride of fast-paced entertainment as well as a fantastically imagined universe full of hard science technology.<br /><br />The writing is so good it takes awhile before you realize the book's chapters consist of a series of tableaus that are sort of a tour of violence in this galaxy. It's exciting! Ok, that came out wrong. Moving on...<br /><br />The science is the best part. All the cool body add-ons. Let's hope our current gene wars in 2011 come out for using and exploring this technology. I'd LOVE glanding! Individual scenes in this book are fast and furious and you won't want to quit reading until the next chapter. <br /><br />Two types of dominant societies, the Culture and Idirans, are both theological-based. The author Banks writes as if a pox on both houses sometimes. But the Culture is the best one for me as I admire humanism, the Culture's theology. The utopian ideals of spreading around happiness by enabling everyone to do their own thing as long as no one is harmed, and promoting science seems the best system to me. I wanted the "hero" Bora Horza Gobuchul, a shapechanger, to die early on since he was on the side of the religious fundamentalist society of the Idirans. The Iderans are evil as far as I am concerned. <br /><br />I had some difficulty connecting with any of the characters (all aliens or modified humans) despite my automatic sympathy with the agents from the Culture, who were fighting the evil Idirans to stop their plan to destroy all societies in the galaxy unless they converted to belief in God - until Unaha-Closp was introduced 2/3rds into the book. <br /><br />The "what happened to..." in the back of the book was hilarious. <br /><br />I didn't know this was a series and this was the first Culture book I read. It is a violent and graphic world, very dark noir with lots of tricky spying and sabotage and war battles and torture and space ships with very cool hard science. Plus there is a mystery race of aliens, the Dra'Azon, that doesn't have much to do with the plot other than having attained a kind of godhood I think. They are a pure energy superspecies which no one seems to notice are godlike, only that they are superior to everybody else in technology. At least so far. <br /><br />I am surprised the Iderans haven't made the Dra'Azons part of their mythology, perhaps as Dark Ones of a sort, since the Iderans believe sentient machines are evil, and the Dra'Azons appear to have the best technology along with having lost their physical form using technology. Isn't that an anathema to religious folk? A presumption of God-like powers in becoming energy beings? Maybe the next books will provide enlightenment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17550115497897504690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_180340422'><a id="like_count_review_180340422" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/180340422?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">27 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F8935689-consider-phlebas">Like</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14845657526705553105">When my friend Arhu recommended this series to me he said it's basically like 4X games and feline wizard did not lie although in first book we can only see that starting to shape out.<br/><br/>So what does this book have in common with those games. Well imagine session of Galactic Civilizations or Alpha Centauri (you have to be bit older to remember this Civilization 2 spin-off) going into late-game where all habitable places and resources are split among players and there are 2 main powers who try to get</span>
  <span id="freeText14845657526705553105" style="display:none">When my friend Arhu recommended this series to me he said it's basically like 4X games and feline wizard did not lie although in first book we can only see that starting to shape out.<br /><br />So what does this book have in common with those games. Well imagine session of Galactic Civilizations or Alpha Centauri (you have to be bit older to remember this Civilization 2 spin-off) going into late-game where all habitable places and resources are split among players and there are 2 main powers who try to get smaller ones by means of diplomacy or war to get edge over other power before final confrontation. This is the sate of the galaxy where book takes place and indeed world building is top notch. Culture as a faction is fascinating, they are anarchistic and hedonistic society where humanoids and AI coexist. Relations between humanoids and AI is superbly well done with some very neat ideas. <br />And while worldbuilding and deserve 5 stars this book isn't getting them because this is also book about shapeshifter Horza's misadventures as Idiran agent in mission to get fugitive mind. Most characters are uninteresting only few get decent characterization, rest are just cardboard cutouts that you will forget about as soon as you turn page.</span>

<div id="review_2137788730" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2137788730" />
    <a title="Simon Fay" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13080935-simon-fay"><img alt="Simon Fay" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443127870p2/13080935.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2137788730?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon Fay" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon Fay" href="/user/show/13080935-simon-fay">Simon Fay</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13080935-simon-fay?shelf=science-fiction-and-fantasy">science-fiction-and-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2137788730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6341491763576544891">I'm rather curious what other people think of the Culture: One of the dominant powers at the forefront of a decades-long galactic war, and, more importantly, the side that Horza, the protagonist of Consider Phlebas, has chosen to fight against.<br/><br/>At the outset, Horza has allied himself with a millennia-old alien species that holds little regard for human life. Horza is a shapeshifter, not entirely human himself, but his biology is a heck of a lot closer to folks like you and me than to the three-le</span>
  <span id="freeText6341491763576544891" style="display:none">I'm rather curious what other people think of the Culture: One of the dominant powers at the forefront of a decades-long galactic war, and, more importantly, the side that Horza, the protagonist of Consider Phlebas, has chosen to fight against.<br /><br />At the outset, Horza has allied himself with a millennia-old alien species that holds little regard for human life. Horza is a shapeshifter, not entirely human himself, but his biology is a heck of a lot closer to folks like you and me than to the three-legged beasts that aim to conquer Earth's greatest empire. At first, I thought his reasoning for working against humanity a little underdeveloped: He didn't seem to have a personal vendetta against the Culture. Yes, he had some ideological issues with them, and a healthy dose of paranoia regarding the artificial minds that run their civilization, but the book never made much of an effort to convince you of the danger that the Culture represents. In fact, at times you wonder why Horza bothered to get involved with such a dangerous conflict at all.<br /><br />I do think the flat characterization is a bit of a fail for Bank's on this occasion. Horza often comes off as a two-dimensional James Bond type. But there are aspects of Consider Phlebas that are much more fleshed out: namely the Culture itself.<br /><br />While Horza treats us to a handful of propaganda lectures regarding the evils and stupidity of the Culture, we are given enough glimpses of their inner workings and the people who populate it to realize they're not necessarily a bad bunch. In general, they come off as kind, educated and enormously empathetic, a quality that works in stark contrast with the meanness that most other characters in the book demonstrate at one time or another. It would have been nice to explore some concrete reasoning behind Horza's paranoia regarding them, but, as it stands, having a protagonist whose goals I didn't agree with did create the interesting effect in me of not knowing how I'd like the story to end. I can't think of another book that left me disappointed, satisfied and relieved all at the same time, but that's what happened here, which more than made up for sections I felt more lacking.<br /><br />Besides all that, Consider Phlebas is a really enjoyable dark space adventure. It's a bit heartless at times, and certainly doesn't try to endear you with loveable characters or romantic quests, but Banks' rich imagination ensures that anybody will find something mindblowing, thought-provoking, or just downright fun within its pages. I look forward to reading the sequels and discovering if Banks will ask me to reevaluate my experience with this first outing... something I strongly suspect he'll do.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6341491763576544891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2605723056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2605723056" />
    <a title="Gary " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27528763-gary"><img alt="Gary " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499717454p2/27528763.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2605723056?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary " href="/user/show/27528763-gary">Gary </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8279737-consider-phlebas">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27528763-gary?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2605723056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12264498838992941484">This novel was good. The author did an excellent job of building a world and creatively fueling it with philosophy and technology. I like large scale space opera ideas when they involve religion, philosophy and the eventual evolution of materialistic ideas both philosophically and realistically. The developed stages of thought of group that were representative of modern day groups was interesting to me. Most importantly, it was character driven.<br/>	The main character, Horza, is a Changer and is abl</span>
  <span id="freeText12264498838992941484" style="display:none">This novel was good. The author did an excellent job of building a world and creatively fueling it with philosophy and technology. I like large scale space opera ideas when they involve religion, philosophy and the eventual evolution of materialistic ideas both philosophically and realistically. The developed stages of thought of group that were representative of modern day groups was interesting to me. Most importantly, it was character driven.<br />	The main character, Horza, is a Changer and is able to assume any shape he wants to which makes him dangerous and coveted by the groups that are vying for power. I like the way his character is rounded out. He is selfish, and can be brutal and callous, yet still has enough of a personality and shows momentary lapses of compassion, that he becomes likable. He is believable, which is saying a lot for a science fiction character than can change shapes like Mystique.<br />	The war itself is good, and serves as more than simply a backdrop to the story. The conflict weaves itself in and out of the thoughts and lives of the characters. Some of the most interesting parts of the story are digressions, like the segment that introduces the primitive civilization on a tropical island. It reminded me more than a little of Brandon in Apocalypse Now cross bred with Dune, and I mean that in a good way.<br />	The pirate excursion to acquire objects works well the first time around, the second time it felt a little like filler. The plot seemed to meander a bit as it moved towards the conclusion, and while I always enjoyed reading it, I did enjoy rushing it a little towards the end. I definitely plan to read the next in the series, just not yet.<br />4.0 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="12264498838992941484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
